<div id="contentSub2"></div>

<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Agricultural developments in 1950s–1960s</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Green_Revolution_(disambiguation)" title="Green Revolution (disambiguation)">Green Revolution (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cropduster_spraying_pesticides.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="433" decoding="async" height="325" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Cropduster_spraying_pesticides.jpg/220px-Cropduster_spraying_pesticides.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Cropduster_spraying_pesticides.jpg/330px-Cropduster_spraying_pesticides.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5e/Cropduster_spraying_pesticides.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cropduster_spraying_pesticides.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>After <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, newly implemented agricultural technologies, including pesticides and fertilizers as well as new breeds of high yield crops, greatly increased food production in certain regions of the Global South.</div></div></div>

template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <b>Green Revolution</b>, or the <b>Third Agricultural Revolution</b> (after the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic_Revolution" title="Neolithic Revolution">Neolithic Revolution</a> and the <a href="/wiki/British_Agricultural_Revolution" title="British Agricultural Revolution">British Agricultural Revolution</a>), is the set of research <a href="/wiki/Technology_transfer" title="Technology transfer">technology transfer</a> initiatives occurring between 1950 and the late 1960s that increased agricultural production in parts of the world, beginning most markedly in the late 1960s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> The initiatives resulted in the adoption of new technologies, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High-yielding_varieties" title="High-yielding varieties">high-yielding varieties</a> (HYVs) of cereals, especially <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dwarf_wheat" title="Dwarf wheat">dwarf wheat</a> and rice. It was associated with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemical_fertilizer" title="Chemical fertilizer">chemical fertilizers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Agrochemical" title="Agrochemical">agrochemicals</a>, and controlled water-supply (usually involving <a href="/wiki/Irrigation" title="Irrigation">irrigation</a>) and newer methods of cultivation, including mechanization. All of these together were seen as a 'package of practices' to supersede 'traditional' technology and to be adopted as a whole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>Both the <a href="/wiki/Ford_Foundation" title="Ford Foundation">Ford Foundation</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Rockefeller_Foundation" title="Rockefeller Foundation">Rockefeller Foundation</a> were heavily involved in its initial development in Mexico.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> One key leader was agricultural scientist <a href="/wiki/Norman_Borlaug" title="Norman Borlaug">Norman Borlaug</a>, the "Father of the Green Revolution", who received the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Peace_Prize" title="Nobel Peace Prize">Nobel Peace Prize</a> in 1970. He is credited with saving over a billion people from starvation. The basic approach was the development of high-yielding varieties of cereal grains, expansion of irrigation infrastructure, modernization of management techniques, distribution of <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)" title="Hybrid (biology)">hybridized seeds</a>, synthetic fertilizers, and <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticides</a> to farmers. As the development of new cereal varieties through selective breeding reached their limits, some agricultural scientists turned to the creation of new strains that did not exist in nature, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_organisms" title="Genetically modified organisms">genetically modified organisms</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>Studies show that the Green Revolution contributed to widespread reduction of poverty, averted hunger for millions, raised incomes, reduced greenhouse gas emissions, reduced land use for agriculture, and contributed to declines in infant mortality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-:5-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p>
<div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Green_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Preliminary_development">Preliminary development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Green_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Preliminary development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/The_Limits_to_Growth" title="The Limits to Growth">The Limits to Growth</a> the first genetic experiments, which a hundred years later resulted in high-yield agricultural crops, took place in a European monastery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Term_.27Green_Revolution.27"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Term_'Green_Revolution'">Term 'Green Revolution'</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Green_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Term 'Green Revolution'">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The term "Green Revolution" was first used by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=William_S._Gaud&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="William S. Gaud (page does not exist)">William S. Gaud</a>, the administrator of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Agency_for_International_Development" title="United States Agency for International Development">U.S. Agency for International Development</a> (USAID), in a speech on 8 March 1968. He noted the spread of the new technologies as: 
</p>
<blockquote><p>"These and other developments in the field of agriculture contain the makings of a new revolution. It is not a violent <a href="/wiki/October_Revolution" title="October Revolution">Red Revolution</a> like that of the Soviets, nor is it a <a href="/wiki/White_Revolution" title="White Revolution">White Revolution</a> like that of the <a href="/wiki/Mohammad_Reza_Pahlavi" title="Mohammad Reza Pahlavi">Shah of Iran</a>. I call it the Green Revolution."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-agbio_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-agbio-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Development_in_Mexico">Development in Mexico</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Green_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Development in Mexico">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Agriculture_in_Mexico" title="Agriculture in Mexico">Agriculture in Mexico</a></div>
<p>Mexico has been called the 'birthplace' and 'burial ground' of the Green Revolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> It began with great promise and it has been argued that "during the twentieth century two 'revolutions' transformed rural Mexico: the <a href="/wiki/Mexican_Revolution" title="Mexican Revolution">Mexican Revolution</a> (1910–1920) and the Green Revolution (1950–1970)."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>It was on the lead of the Mexican government in 1943, under Presidential order and finance of the Mexican President <a href="/wiki/Manuel_%C3%81vila_Camacho" title="Manuel Ávila Camacho">Manuel Ávila Camacho</a>, and support of the U.S. government, the United Nations, the <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Agriculture_Organization" title="Food and Agriculture Organization">Food and Agriculture Organization</a> (FAO), and the <a href="/wiki/Rockefeller_Foundation" title="Rockefeller Foundation">Rockefeller Foundation</a>. For the U.S. government, its neighbor Mexico was an important experimental case in the use of technology and scientific expertise in agriculture that became the model for international agricultural development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Mexico made a concerted effort to transform agricultural productivity, particularly with irrigated rather than dry-land cultivation in its northwest, to solve its problem of lack of food self-sufficiency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> In the center and south of Mexico, where large-scale production faced challenges, agricultural production languished.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Increased production promised food <a href="/wiki/Self-sustainability" title="Self-sustainability">self-sufficiency</a> in Mexico to feed its growing and urbanizing population with the increase in a number of calories consumed per Mexican.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> The technology was seen as a valuable way to feed the poor and would relieve some pressure of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Land_redistribution" title="Land redistribution">land redistribution</a> process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> In general, the success of "Green Revolution" depended on the use of machinery for cultivation and harvest, on large-scale agricultural enterprises with access to credit (often from foreign investors), government-supported infrastructure projects, and access to low-wage agricultural workers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mexico was the recipient of knowledge and technology of the Green Revolution, and it was an active participant with financial supports from the government for agriculture and Mexican agronomists. In the aftermath of the Mexican Revolution, the government had redistributed land to peasants in some parts of the country which had broken the back of the <a href="/wiki/Hacienda" title="Hacienda">hacienda</a> system. During the presidency of <a href="/wiki/L%C3%A1zaro_C%C3%A1rdenas" title="Lázaro Cárdenas">Lázaro Cárdenas</a> (1934-1940), <a href="/wiki/Land_reform_in_Mexico" title="Land reform in Mexico">land reform in Mexico</a> reached its apex in the center and south of Mexico. Agricultural productivity had fallen significantly by the 1940s. American Vice President <a href="/wiki/Henry_A._Wallace" title="Henry A. Wallace">Henry A. Wallace</a>, previously president <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Franklin_Delano_Roosevelt" title="Franklin Delano Roosevelt">Franklin Delano Roosevelt</a>'s Secretary of Agriculture, visited Mexico who helped in uplifting the research program in Mexico that emphasized in increased productivity rather than land reform.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the administration of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manuel_Avila_Camacho" title="Manuel Avila Camacho">Manuel Avila Camacho</a> (1940–46), the government put resources into developing new breeds of plants and partnered with the <a href="/wiki/Rockefeller_Foundation" title="Rockefeller Foundation">Rockefeller Foundation</a>, and was also supported by the U.S. Department of Agriculture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cotter,_p._11_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cotter,_p._11-24">[24]</a></sup> In 1941, a team of U.S. scientists, Richard Bradfield (Cornell University), <a href="/wiki/Paul_Christoph_Mangelsdorf" title="Paul Christoph Mangelsdorf">Paul C. Mangelsdorf</a> (Harvard University), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elvin_Charles_Stakman" title="Elvin Charles Stakman">Elvin Charles Stakman</a> (University of Minnesota) surveyed Mexican agriculture to recommend policies and practices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Norman_Borlaug" title="Norman Borlaug">Norman Borlaug</a>, a key figure developing Green Revolution practices in Mexico, studied with Stakman at University of Minnesota.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uom_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-uom-26">[26]</a></sup>  In 1943, the Mexican government founded the <a href="/wiki/International_Maize_and_Wheat_Improvement_Center" title="International Maize and Wheat Improvement Center">International Maize and Wheat Improvement Center</a> (CIMMYT), which became a base for international agricultural research. 
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Borlaug_Mexico_locations.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="323" data-file-width="633" decoding="async" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Borlaug_Mexico_locations.png/300px-Borlaug_Mexico_locations.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Borlaug_Mexico_locations.png/450px-Borlaug_Mexico_locations.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Borlaug_Mexico_locations.png/600px-Borlaug_Mexico_locations.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Borlaug_Mexico_locations.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Locations of Norman Borlaug's research stations in the Yaqui Valley and Chapingo.</div></div></div>
<p>Agriculture in Mexico had been a sociopolitical issue, a key factor in some regions' participation in the Mexican Revolution. It was also a technical issue enabled by a cohort of trained agronomists who advised peasants how to increase productivity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> In the post-World War II era, the government sought development in agriculture that bettered technological aspects of agriculture in regions—not dominated by small-scale peasant cultivators.  This drive for agricultural transformation would have the benefit to Mexico on self-sufficiency in food and in the political sphere during the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> (potentially stem unrest and the appeal of Communism).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cotter,_p._11_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cotter,_p._11-24">[24]</a></sup> Technical aid can also be seen as serving political ends in the international sphere. In Mexico, it also served political ends separating peasant agriculture based on the <a href="/wiki/Ejido" title="Ejido">ejido</a> and considered one of the victories of the Mexican Revolution,  from agribusiness that requires large-scale land ownership, irrigation, specialized seeds, fertilizers, and pesticides, machinery, and a low-wage paid labor force.
</p><p>The Mexican government created the Mexican Agricultural Program (MAP) to be the lead organization in raising productivity. One of their successes was in wheat production with varieties dominating wheat production as early as 1951 (70%), 1965 (80%), and 1968 (90%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Mexico became the showcase for extending the Green Revolution to other areas of Latin America and beyond, into Africa and Asia. New breeds of maize, beans, and wheat produced bumper crops with proper inputs (such as fertilizer and pesticides) and careful cultivation. Many Mexican farmers who had been dubious about the scientists or hostile to them (often a mutual relationship of discord) came to see the scientific approach to agriculture as worth adopting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>The requirement for the full package of inputs of new strains of seeds, fertilizer, synthetic pesticides, and water were often not within the reach of small-scale farmers.  The application of pesticides could be hazardous for farmers. Their use often damaged the local ecology, contaminating waterways and endangering the health of workers and newborns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>One of the participants in the Mexican experiment, Edwin J. Wellhausen, summarized the factors leading to its initial success. These include: high yield plants without  disease resistivity, adaptability, and ability to utilize fertilizers; improved use of soils, adequate fertilizers, and control of weeds and pests; and "a favorable ratio between the cost of fertilizers (and other investments) to the price of the produce."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="IR8_rice_and_the_Philippines">IR8 rice and the Philippines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Green_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: IR8 rice and the Philippines">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1960, Bridgett B the Government of the Republic of the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Ford_Foundation" title="Ford Foundation">Ford Foundation</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Rockefeller_Foundation" title="Rockefeller Foundation">Rockefeller Foundation</a> established the <a href="/wiki/International_Rice_Research_Institute" title="International Rice Research Institute">International Rice Research Institute</a> (IRRI). A <a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">rice</a> crossing between Dee-Geo-woo-gen and Peta was done at IRRI in 1962. In 1966, one of the breeding lines became a new <a href="/wiki/Cultivar" title="Cultivar">cultivar</a>: <a href="/wiki/IR8" title="IR8">IR8 rice</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ir8_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-ir8-32">[32]</a></sup>  IR8 required the use of fertilizers and pesticides, but produced substantially higher yields than the traditional cultivars. Annual rice production in the Philippines increased from 3.7 to 7.7 million tons in two decades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> The switch to IR8 rice made the Philippines a rice exporter for the first time in the 20th century,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> though imports still exceeded exports, according to data from the United Nations Food and Agriculture Organization. From 1966 to 1986, the Philippines imported around 2,679,000 metric tons and exported only 632,000 metric tons of milled rice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Start_in_India">Start in India</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Green_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Start in India">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Green_Revolution_in_India" title="Green Revolution in India">Green Revolution in India</a></div>
<p>In 1961, India was on the brink of mass famine.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="existing reference does not fully support the statement that India was on the brink of mass famine in 1961 (February 2017)">additional citation(s) needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Norman_Borlaug" title="Norman Borlaug">Norman Borlaug</a> was invited to India by the adviser to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minister_of_Agriculture_(India)" title="Minister of Agriculture (India)">Indian Minister of Agriculture</a> Dr. <a href="/wiki/M._S._Swaminathan" title="M. S. Swaminathan">M. S. Swaminathan</a>. Despite bureaucratic hurdles imposed by India's grain monopolies, the Ford Foundation and Indian government collaborated to import wheat seed from the <a href="/wiki/International_Maize_and_Wheat_Improvement_Center" title="International Maize and Wheat Improvement Center">International Maize and Wheat Improvement Center</a> (CIMMYT). The state of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Punjab_(India)" title="Punjab (India)">Punjab</a> was selected by the Indian government to be the first site to try the new crops because of its reliable water supply, the presence of Indus plains which make it one of the most fertile plains on earth, and a history of agricultural success. India began its own Green Revolution program of plant breeding, irrigation development, and financing of agrochemicals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>India soon adopted <a href="/wiki/IR8" title="IR8">IR8</a>—a semi-dwarf rice variety developed by the <a href="/wiki/International_Rice_Research_Institute" title="International Rice Research Institute">International Rice Research Institute</a> (IRRI) that could produce more grains of rice per plant when grown with certain fertilizers and irrigation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> In 1968, Indian agronomist <a href="/wiki/Surajit_Kumar_De_Datta" title="Surajit Kumar De Datta">S.K. De Datta</a> published his findings that IR8 rice yielded about 5 tons per hectare with no fertilizer, and almost 10 tons per hectare under optimal conditions. This was 10 times the yield of traditional rice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> IR8 was a success throughout Asia, and dubbed the "Miracle Rice." IR8 was also developed into <a href="/wiki/Semi-dwarf_IR36" title="Semi-dwarf IR36">Semi-dwarf IR36</a>.
</p><p>In the 1960s, rice yields in India were about two tons per hectare; by the mid-1990s, they had risen to 6 tons per hectare. In the 1970s, rice cost about $550 a ton; in 2001, it cost under $200 a ton.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> India became one of the world's most successful rice producers, and is now a major rice exporter, shipping nearly 4.5 million tons in 2006.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Green_Revolution_in_China">Green Revolution in China</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Green_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Green Revolution in China">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>China's Green Revolution came from its own fruition, and cannot necessarily be credited to practices popularized by Norman Borlaug. China's large and increasing population meant that increasing food production, principally rice, was a top priority for the Chinese government. When the People's Republic of China was established in 1949, the Chinese Communist Party made it a priority to pursue agricultural development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-:6-41">[41]</a></sup> They sought to solve China's food security issues by focusing on traditional crop production, the implementation of modern technology and science, creating food reserves for the population, high-yield seed varieties, multi-cropping, controlled irrigation, and protecting food security.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-:7-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-:6-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> This began with the Agrarian Reform Law of 1950, which ended private land ownership and gave land back to the peasants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_41-2"><a href="#cite_note-:6-41">[41]</a></sup> The beginning of China's Green Revolution is marked by the government's sponsorship of agricultural research, specifically in producing a high-yielding rice variety for the rapidly growing population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_41-3"><a href="#cite_note-:6-41">[41]</a></sup> These efforts began during the <a href="/wiki/Great_Leap_Forward" title="Great Leap Forward">Great Leap Forward</a>, a time from 1959 to 1961 where the Government launched a campaign to reconstruct their agrarian economy into a communist society and established the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_commune" title="People's commune">People's Commune</a>.
</p><p>Prominent in the development of productive hybrid rice was <a href="/wiki/Yuan_Longping" title="Yuan Longping">Yuan Longping</a>, whose research hybridized wild strains of rice with existing strains.  He has been dubbed “the father of hybrid rice,”<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> and was considered a national hero in China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Chinese rice production met the nation's food security needs, and today they are a leading exporter of rice.  In recent years, however, extensive use of ground water for irrigation has drawn down aquifers and extensive use of fertilizers has increased greenhouse gas emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> China has not expanded the area of cultivable land, but the Green Revolution with high yields per hectare gave China the food security it sought.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1979, there were 490 million Chinese people living in poverty. In 2014, there were only 82 million. China's population had once been 50% of it hungry and in poverty, but by 2014, they were down to only 6%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-:7-42">[42]</a></sup> If China's stats were to be excluded entirely from Green Revolution studies, they would find that world hunger actually increased.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_42-2"><a href="#cite_note-:7-42">[42]</a></sup> Unlike in China, where locally grown produce would stay within local markets, the food in other countries was being placed on the global market, never to be eaten by those who grew it.
</p>
<h3><span id="Brazil.27s_agricultural_revolution"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Brazil's_agricultural_revolution">Brazil's agricultural revolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Green_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Brazil's agricultural revolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Brazil's vast inland <a href="/wiki/Cerrado" title="Cerrado">cerrado</a> region was regarded as unfit for farming before the 1960s because the soil was too acidic and poor in nutrients, according to <a href="/wiki/Norman_Borlaug" title="Norman Borlaug">Norman Borlaug</a>. However, from the 1960s, vast quantities of lime (pulverised chalk or <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a>) were poured on the soil to reduce acidity. The effort went on for decades; by the late 1990s, between 14 million and 16 million tonnes of lime were being spread on Brazilian fields each year. The quantity rose to 25 million tonnes in 2003 and 2004, equalling around five tonnes of lime per hectare. As a result, Brazil has become the world's second biggest <a href="/wiki/Soybean" title="Soybean">soybean</a> exporter. Soybeans are also widely used in animal feed, and the large volume of soy produced in Brazil has contributed to Brazil's rise to become the biggest exporter of beef and poultry in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Several parallels can also be found in Argentina's boom in soybean production as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Problems_in_Africa">Problems in Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Green_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Problems in Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There have been numerous attempts to introduce the successful concepts from the Mexican and Indian projects into Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> These programs have generally been less successful. Reasons cited include widespread corruption, insecurity, a lack of infrastructure, and a general lack of will on the part of the governments. Yet environmental factors, such as the availability of water for irrigation, the high diversity in slope and soil types in one given area are also reasons why the Green Revolution is not so successful in Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inwent.org_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-inwent.org-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>A recent program in western Africa is attempting to introduce a new high-yielding 'family' of rice varieties known as "<a href="/wiki/New_Rice_for_Africa" title="New Rice for Africa">New Rice for Africa</a>" (NERICA). NERICA varieties yield about 30% more rice under normal conditions, and can double yields with small amounts of fertilizer and very basic irrigation. However, the program has been beset by problems getting the rice into the hands of farmers, and to date the only success has been in <a href="/wiki/Guinea" title="Guinea">Guinea</a>, where it currently accounts for 16% of rice cultivation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>After a famine in 2001 and years of chronic hunger and poverty, in 2005 the small African country of <a href="/wiki/Malawi" title="Malawi">Malawi</a> launched the "Agricultural Input Subsidy Program" by which vouchers are given to smallholder farmers to buy subsidized nitrogen fertilizer and maize seeds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Within its first year, the program was reported to have had extreme success, producing the largest maize harvest of the country's history, enough to feed the country with tons of maize left over. The program has advanced yearly ever since. Various sources claim that the program has been an unusual success, hailing it as a "miracle".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Malawi experienced a 40% drop in maize production in 2015 and 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oakland-currier_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-oakland-currier-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2021 randomized control trial on temporary subsidies for maize farmers in Mozambique found that adoption of Green Revolution technology led to increased maize yields in both the short- and long-term.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Consultative_Group_on_International_Agricultural_Research">Consultative Group on International Agricultural Research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Green_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Consultative Group on International Agricultural Research">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1970, foundation officials proposed a worldwide network of agricultural research centers under a permanent secretariat. This was further supported and developed by the <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a>; on 19 May 1971, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Consultative_Group_on_International_Agricultural_Research" title="Consultative Group on International Agricultural Research">Consultative Group on International Agricultural Research</a> (CGIAR) was established, co-sponsored by the <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Agriculture_Organization" title="Food and Agriculture Organization">FAO</a>, <a href="/wiki/International_Fund_for_Agricultural_Development" title="International Fund for Agricultural Development">IFAD</a>, and <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Development_Programme" title="United Nations Development Programme">UNDP</a>. CGIAR has added many research centers throughout the world.
</p><p>CGIAR has responded, at least in part, to criticisms of Green Revolution methodologies. This began in the 1980s, and mainly was a result of pressure from donor organizations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oasa_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-oasa-57">[57]</a></sup> Methods like <a href="/wiki/Agroecosystem_analysis" title="Agroecosystem analysis">agroecosystem analysis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Farming_systems_in_India" title="Farming systems in India">farming system</a> research have been adopted to gain a more holistic view of agriculture.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Agricultural_production_and_food_security">Agricultural production and food security</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Green_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Agricultural production and food security">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>According to a 2012 review in <i>Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences</i> of the existing academic literature, the Green Revolution "contributed to widespread poverty reduction, averted hunger for millions of people, and avoided the conversion of thousands of hectares of land into agricultural cultivation."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Technologies">Technologies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Green_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Technologies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wheat-haHula-ISRAEL2.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2055" data-file-width="2773" decoding="async" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Wheat-haHula-ISRAEL2.JPG/220px-Wheat-haHula-ISRAEL2.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Wheat-haHula-ISRAEL2.JPG/330px-Wheat-haHula-ISRAEL2.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Wheat-haHula-ISRAEL2.JPG/440px-Wheat-haHula-ISRAEL2.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wheat-haHula-ISRAEL2.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>New varieties of wheat and other grains were instrumental to the green revolution.</div></div></div>
<p>The Green Revolution spread technologies that already existed, but had not been widely implemented outside industrialized nations. Two kinds of technologies were used in the Green Revolution and aim at cultivation and breeding area respectively. The technologies in cultivation are targeted at providing excellent growing conditions, which included modern <a href="/wiki/Irrigation" title="Irrigation">irrigation</a> projects, <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticides</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synthetic_nitrogen_fertilizer" title="Synthetic nitrogen fertilizer">synthetic nitrogen fertilizer</a>. The breeding technologies aimed at improving crop varieties developed through the conventional, science-based methods available at the time. These technologies included <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)" title="Hybrid (biology)">hybrids</a>, combining modern genetics with selections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="High-yielding_varieties">High-yielding varieties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Green_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: High-yielding varieties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The novel technological development of the Green Revolution was the production of novel wheat <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cultivars" title="Cultivars">cultivars</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agronomist" title="Agronomist">Agronomists</a> <a href="/wiki/Plant_breeding" title="Plant breeding">bred</a> cultivars of maize, wheat, and rice that are the generally referred to as HYVs or "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High-yielding_varieties" title="High-yielding varieties">high-yielding varieties</a>". HYVs have higher nitrogen-absorbing potential than other varieties. Since cereals that absorbed extra nitrogen would typically lodge, or fall over before harvest, semi-dwarfing <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">genes</a> were bred into their <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">genomes</a>. A Japanese dwarf wheat cultivar <a href="/wiki/Norin_10_wheat" title="Norin 10 wheat">Norin 10</a> developed by Japanese agronomist Gonjiro Inazuka, which was sent to <a href="/wiki/Orville_Vogel" title="Orville Vogel">Orville Vogel</a> at <a href="/wiki/Washington_State_University" title="Washington State University">Washington State University</a> by <a href="/wiki/Cecil_Salmon" title="Cecil Salmon">Cecil Salmon</a>, was instrumental in developing Green Revolution wheat cultivars. IR8, the first widely implemented HYV rice to be developed by IRRI, was created through a cross between an Indonesian variety named "Peta" and a Chinese variety named "Dee-geo-woo-gen".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> In the 1960s, when a food crisis happened in Asia, the spread of HYV rice was aggravated intensely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>Dr. <a href="/wiki/Norman_Borlaug" title="Norman Borlaug">Norman Borlaug</a>, who is usually recognized as the "Father of the Green Revolution", bred rust-resistant cultivars which have strong and firm stems, preventing them from falling over under extreme weather at high levels of fertilization. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CIMMYT" title="CIMMYT">CIMMYT</a>(Centro Internacional de Mejoramiento de Maiz y Trigo – International Center for Maize and Wheat Improvements) conducted these breeding programs and helped spread <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High-yielding_varieties" title="High-yielding varieties">high-yielding varieties</a> in Mexico and countries in Asia like India and Pakistan. These programs successfully led the harvest double in these countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>Plant scientists figured out several parameters related to the high yield and identified the related genes which control the plant height and tiller number.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> With advances in <a href="/wiki/Molecular_genetics" title="Molecular genetics">molecular genetics</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Mutant" title="Mutant">mutant</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genes" title="Genes">genes</a> responsible for <i><a href="/wiki/Arabidopsis_thaliana" title="Arabidopsis thaliana">Arabidopsis thaliana</a></i> genes (GA 20-oxidase,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> <i>ga1</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> <i>ga1-3</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>), wheat reduced-height genes (<i>Rht</i>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>  and  a rice semidwarf gene (<i>sd1</i>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cloned" title="Cloned">cloned</a>. These were identified as <a href="/wiki/Gibberellin" title="Gibberellin">gibberellin</a> <a href="/wiki/Biosynthesis" title="Biosynthesis">biosynthesis</a> genes or <a href="/wiki/Cell_signaling" title="Cell signaling">cellular signaling</a> component genes. <a href="/wiki/Plant_stem" title="Plant stem">Stem</a> growth in the mutant background is significantly reduced leading to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dwarf_plant" title="Dwarf plant">dwarf</a> <a href="/wiki/Phenotype" title="Phenotype">phenotype</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photosynthetic" title="Photosynthetic">Photosynthetic</a> investment in the stem is reduced dramatically as the shorter plants are inherently more stable mechanically. Assimilates become redirected to grain production, amplifying in particular the effect of chemical fertilizers on commercial yield.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>HYVs significantly outperform traditional varieties in the presence of adequate irrigation, pesticides, and fertilizers. In the absence of these inputs, traditional varieties may outperform HYVs. Therefore, several authors have challenged the apparent superiority of HYVs not only compared to the traditional varieties alone, but by contrasting the monocultural system associated with HYVs with the polycultural system associated with traditional ones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Production_increases">Production increases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Green_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Production increases">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wheat_yields_in_Least_Developed_Countries.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="550" data-file-width="700" decoding="async" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Wheat_yields_in_Least_Developed_Countries.svg/220px-Wheat_yields_in_Least_Developed_Countries.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Wheat_yields_in_Least_Developed_Countries.svg/330px-Wheat_yields_in_Least_Developed_Countries.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Wheat_yields_in_Least_Developed_Countries.svg/440px-Wheat_yields_in_Least_Developed_Countries.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wheat_yields_in_Least_Developed_Countries.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Wheat yields in <a href="/wiki/Least_developed_countries" title="Least developed countries">least developed countries</a> since 1961, in kilograms per hectare.</div></div></div>
<p>By one 2021 estimate, the Green Revolution increased yields by 44% between 1965 and 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-8">[8]</a></sup> Cereal production more than doubled in developing nations between the years 1961–1985.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-conway1_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-conway1-68">[68]</a></sup> Yields of rice, maize, and wheat increased steadily during that period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-conway1_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-conway1-68">[68]</a></sup> The production increases can be attributed roughly equally to irrigation, fertilizer, and seed development, at least in the case of Asian rice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-conway1_68-2"><a href="#cite_note-conway1-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>While agricultural output increased as a result of the Green Revolution, the energy input to produce a crop has increased faster,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>  so that the ratio of crops produced to energy input has decreased over time. Green Revolution techniques also heavily rely on <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_machinery" title="Agricultural machinery">agricultural machinery</a> and chemical <a href="/wiki/Fertilizer" title="Fertilizer">fertilizers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Herbicide" title="Herbicide">herbicides</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Defoliant" title="Defoliant">defoliants</a>; which, as of 2014, rely on or are derived from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crude_oil" title="Crude oil">crude oil</a>, making agriculture increasingly reliant on crude oil extraction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>  Proponents of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peak_Oil" title="Peak Oil">Peak Oil</a> theory fear that a future decline in oil and gas production would lead to a decline in food production or even a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Malthusian_catastrophe" title="Malthusian catastrophe">Malthusian catastrophe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:World_population_history.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="450" data-file-width="750" decoding="async" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/World_population_history.svg/220px-World_population_history.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/World_population_history.svg/330px-World_population_history.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/World_population_history.svg/440px-World_population_history.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:World_population_history.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>World population 1950–2010</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_on_food_security">Effects on food security</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Green_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Effects on food security">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Food_security" title="Food security">Food security</a></div>
<p>The energy for the Green Revolution was provided by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fossil_fuels" title="Fossil fuels">fossil fuels</a> in the form of fertilizers (natural gas), pesticides (oil), and <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbon</a> fueled <a href="/wiki/Irrigation" title="Irrigation">irrigation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> The development of synthetic nitrogen fertilizer has significantly supported global <a href="/wiki/Population_growth" title="Population growth">population growth</a> — it has been estimated that almost half the people on the Earth are currently fed as a result of synthetic nitrogen fertilizer use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> According to ICIS Fertilizers managing editor Julia Meehan, "People don’t realise that 50% of the world’s food relies on fertilisers."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/World_population" title="World population">world population</a> has grown by about five billion<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> since the beginning of the Green Revolution and many believe that, without the Revolution, there would have been greater <a href="/wiki/Famine" title="Famine">famine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malnutrition" title="Malnutrition">malnutrition</a>. India saw annual wheat production rise from 10 million tons in the 1960s to 73 million in 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> The average person in the developing world consumes roughly 25% more calories per day now than before the Green Revolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-conway1_68-3"><a href="#cite_note-conway1-68">[68]</a></sup>  Between 1950 and 1984, as the Green Revolution transformed agriculture around the globe, world grain production increased by about 160%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p>The production increases fostered by the Green Revolution are often credited with having helped to avoid widespread <a href="/wiki/Famine" title="Famine">famine</a>, and for feeding billions of people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are also claims that the Green Revolution has decreased food security for a large number of people. One claim involves the shift of subsistence-oriented cropland to cropland oriented towards production of grain for export or animal feed. For example, the Green Revolution replaced much of the land used for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pulse_(legume)" title="Pulse (legume)">pulses</a> that fed Indian peasants for wheat, which did not make up a large portion of the peasant diet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spitz_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-spitz-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Food_security">Food security</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Green_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Food security">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:World_population_supported_by_synthetic_nitrogen_fertilizers,_OWID.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="850" decoding="async" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/World_population_supported_by_synthetic_nitrogen_fertilizers%2C_OWID.svg/220px-World_population_supported_by_synthetic_nitrogen_fertilizers%2C_OWID.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/World_population_supported_by_synthetic_nitrogen_fertilizers%2C_OWID.svg/330px-World_population_supported_by_synthetic_nitrogen_fertilizers%2C_OWID.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/World_population_supported_by_synthetic_nitrogen_fertilizers%2C_OWID.svg/440px-World_population_supported_by_synthetic_nitrogen_fertilizers%2C_OWID.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:World_population_supported_by_synthetic_nitrogen_fertilizers,_OWID.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/World_population" title="World population">World population</a> supported with and without synthetic nitrogen fertilizers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Malthusian_criticism">Malthusian criticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Green_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Malthusian criticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Some criticisms generally involve some variation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Malthusian" title="Malthusian">Malthusian</a> principle of population. Such concerns often revolve around the idea that the Green Revolution is unsustainable,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> and argue that humanity is now in a state of <a href="/wiki/Human_overpopulation" title="Human overpopulation">overpopulation</a> or <a href="/wiki/Overshoot_(population)" title="Overshoot (population)">overshoot</a> with regards to the sustainable <a href="/wiki/Carrying_capacity#Humans" title="Carrying capacity">carrying capacity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ecological_footprint" title="Ecological footprint">ecological demands</a> on the Earth. A 2021 study found, contrary to the expectations of the Malthusian hypothesis, that the Green Revolution led to reduced population growth, rather than an increase in population growth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-:1-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although 36 million people die each year as a direct or indirect result of hunger and poor nutrition,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Wikipedia_and_sources_that_mirror_or_use_it" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="This claim cites another Wikipedia article. Articles need references to reliable third-party sources. (August 2020)">circular reference</span></a></i>]</sup> Malthus's more extreme predictions have frequently failed to materialize. In 1798 Thomas Malthus made his prediction of impending famine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Green_Revolutionary_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Green_Revolutionary-84">[84]</a></sup>  The world's population had doubled by 1923 and doubled again by 1973 without fulfilling Malthus's prediction. Malthusian <a href="/wiki/Paul_R._Ehrlich" title="Paul R. Ehrlich">Paul R. Ehrlich</a>, in his 1968 book <i><a href="/wiki/The_Population_Bomb" title="The Population Bomb">The Population Bomb</a></i>, said that "India couldn't possibly feed two hundred million more people by 1980" and "Hundreds of millions of people will starve to death in spite of any crash programs."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Green_Revolutionary_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-Green_Revolutionary-84">[84]</a></sup>  Ehrlich's warnings failed to materialize when India became self-sustaining in cereal production in 1974 (six years later) as a result of the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Norman_Borlaug" title="Norman Borlaug">Norman Borlaug</a>'s dwarf wheat varieties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Green_Revolutionary_84-2"><a href="#cite_note-Green_Revolutionary-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, Borlaug was well aware of the implications of population growth. In his Nobel lecture he repeatedly presented improvements in food production within a sober understanding of the context of population. "The green revolution has won a temporary success in man's war against hunger and deprivation; it has given man a breathing space. If fully implemented, the revolution can provide sufficient food for sustenance during the next three decades. But the frightening power of human reproduction must also be curbed; otherwise the success of the green revolution will be ephemeral only. Most people still fail to comprehend the magnitude and menace of the "Population Monster"...Since man is potentially a rational being, however, I am confident that within the next two decades he will recognize the self-destructive course he steers along the road of irresponsible population growth..."
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hubbert_peak_oil_plot.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Hubbert_peak_oil_plot.svg/220px-Hubbert_peak_oil_plot.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Hubbert_peak_oil_plot.svg/330px-Hubbert_peak_oil_plot.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Hubbert_peak_oil_plot.svg/440px-Hubbert_peak_oil_plot.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hubbert_peak_oil_plot.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/M._King_Hubbert" title="M. King Hubbert">M. King Hubbert</a>'s prediction of world petroleum production rates. Modern agriculture is largely reliant on petroleum energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Famine">Famine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Green_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Famine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>To some modern Western sociologists and writers, increasing food production is not synonymous with increasing <a href="/wiki/Food_security" title="Food security">food security</a>, and is only part of a larger equation. For example, Harvard professor <a href="/wiki/Amartya_Sen" title="Amartya Sen">Amartya Sen</a> wrote that large <a href="/wiki/List_of_famines" title="List of famines">historic famines</a> were not caused by decreases in food supply, but by socioeconomic dynamics and a failure of public action.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sen_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-sen-86">[86]</a></sup> Economist <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Peter_Bowbrick&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Peter Bowbrick (page does not exist)">Peter Bowbrick</a> disputes Sen's theory, arguing that Sen relies on inconsistent arguments and contradicts available information, including sources that Sen himself cited.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowbrick1986_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bowbrick1986-87">[87]</a></sup> Bowbrick further argues that Sen's views coincide with that of the <a href="/wiki/Bengal" title="Bengal">Bengal</a> government at the time of the <a href="/wiki/Bengal_famine_of_1943" title="Bengal famine of 1943">Bengal famine of 1943</a>, and the policies Sen advocates failed to relieve the famine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowbrick1986_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bowbrick1986-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Quality_of_diet">Quality of diet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Green_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Quality of diet">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Some have challenged the value of the increased food production of Green Revolution agriculture. <a href="/wiki/Miguel_Altieri" title="Miguel Altieri">Miguel A. Altieri</a>, (a pioneer of agroecology and peasant-advocate), writes that the comparison between traditional systems of agriculture and Green Revolution agriculture has been unfair, because Green Revolution agriculture produces <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monocultures" title="Monocultures">monocultures</a> of cereal grains, while traditional agriculture usually incorporates <a href="/wiki/Polyculture" title="Polyculture">polycultures</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>These monoculture crops are often used for export, feed for animals, or conversion into biofuel. According to Emile Frison of <a href="/wiki/Bioversity_International" title="Bioversity International">Bioversity International</a>, the Green Revolution has also led to a change in dietary habits, as fewer people are affected by hunger and die from starvation, but many are affected by <a href="/wiki/Malnutrition" title="Malnutrition">malnutrition</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Iron_deficiency" title="Iron deficiency">iron</a> or <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_A_deficiency" title="Vitamin A deficiency">vitamin-A deficiencies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inwent.org_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-inwent.org-51">[51]</a></sup> Frison further asserts that almost 60% of yearly deaths of children under age five in developing countries are related to malnutrition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inwent.org_51-2"><a href="#cite_note-inwent.org-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>The strategies developed by the Green Revolution focused on fending off starvation and was very successful in raising overall yields of cereal grains, but did not give sufficient relevance to nutritional quality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SandsMorris2009_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-SandsMorris2009-88">[88]</a></sup> High yield-cereal crops have low <a href="/wiki/Protein_quality" title="Protein quality">quality proteins</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Essential_amino_acid" title="Essential amino acid">essential amino acid</a> deficiencies, are high in <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a>, and lack balanced <a href="/wiki/Essential_fatty_acid" title="Essential fatty acid">essential fatty acids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vitamin" title="Vitamin">vitamins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mineral" title="Mineral">minerals</a> and other quality factors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SandsMorris2009_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-SandsMorris2009-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>High-yield rice (HYR), introduced since 1964 to poverty-ridden Asian countries, such as the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>, was found to have inferior flavor and be more glutinous and less savory than their native varieties.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> This caused its price to be lower than the average market value.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the Philippines the introduction of heavy pesticides to rice production, in the early part of the Green Revolution, poisoned and killed off fish and weedy green vegetables that traditionally coexisted in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rice_paddy" title="Rice paddy">rice paddies</a>. These were nutritious food sources for many poor Filipino farmers prior to the introduction of pesticides, further impacting the diets of locals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rice-robbery_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-rice-robbery-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_impact">Political impact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Green_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Political impact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>A critic<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> of the Green Revolution, American <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Investigative_journalist" title="Investigative journalist">journalist</a> Mark Dowie argues that "the primary objective of the program was geopolitical: to provide food for the populace in undeveloped countries and so bring social stability and weaken the fomenting of communist insurgency."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> Citing internal Foundation documents, Dowie states that the Ford Foundation had a greater concern than Rockefeller in this area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Socioeconomic_impacts">Socioeconomic impacts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Green_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Socioeconomic impacts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The transition from traditional agriculture (in which inputs were generated on-farm) to Green Revolution agriculture (which required the purchase of inputs) led to the widespread establishment of rural credit institutions. Smaller farmers often went into <a href="/wiki/Debt" title="Debt">debt</a>, which in many cases resulted in a loss of their farmland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oasa_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-oasa-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ponting_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ponting-94">[94]</a></sup>  The increased level of mechanization on larger farms made possible by the Green Revolution removed a large source of employment from the rural economy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oasa_57-2"><a href="#cite_note-oasa-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>The new <a href="/wiki/Socioeconomics" title="Socioeconomics">economic</a> difficulties of <a href="/wiki/Smallholding" title="Smallholding">smallholder</a> farmers and landless farm workers led to increased <a href="/wiki/Urbanization" title="Urbanization">rural-urban migration</a>. The increase in food production led to cheaper food for urban dwellers.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>According to a 2021 study, the Green Revolution substantially increased income.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_8-3"><a href="#cite_note-:1-8">[8]</a></sup> A delay in the Green Revolution by ten years would have cost 17% of GDP per capita, whereas if the Green Revolution had never happened, it could have reduced GDP per capita in the developing world by half.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_8-4"><a href="#cite_note-:1-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_impact">Environmental impact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Green_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Environmental impact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
